CHAPTER VIII

SUDDHI

Åšuddhi (purification ) is a very comprehensive topic including within it purification after ÄÅ›auca (impurity on birth and death), purification of a person after contact with an impure object or on account of certain occurrences. purification of pots, wells, food &c., after they are polluted. AÅ›auca, however, is the most important subject under Å›uddhi and therefore the Åšuddhi.kaumudi ‘defines ‘Å›uddhi’ as ’the state of being fit for or capable of performing the rites that are understood from the Veda’1. The Smá¹›tis employ the word Å›uddhi in relation to purification after ÄÅ›auca. For example, Manu V.57 starts by saying that he will expound purification after a man is dead ( pretaÅ›uddhi) and purification of things (dravyaÅ›uddhi). Manu V. 83 ( = Daká¹£a VI. 7) avers that a brÄhmaá¹‡a becomes pure after ten days (on the death or birth of a relative), a ká¹£atriya after twelve &c. The ParÄÅ›ara-smá¹›ti starts chapter three by declaring ‘I shall expound Å›uddhi on birth and death.’ Yaj. III. 14, 25 also employ the word Å›uddhi. Therefore aÅ›auca on birth and death will be first dealt with.

ÄÅ›auca

The word ÄÅ›auca is formed from suci (pure) according to Pan. V. 1.131 2 and PÄn. VII. 3, 30 (vide note below) with the negative particle na (a). In some smá¹›tis such as that of Devala the form ‘ÄÅ›ucya’ also is found (see HÄralatÄ pp. 2, 9, 36 in quotations from Devala[596b]). Another word which is often used as a synonym for ÄÅ›auca is ‘agha’. It has been seen above (p. 6) that the word agha means ‘sin’ in the Vedic Literature (as in á¹šg. I. 97, 1-8, X. 117.6). But in the SÄn, Åšr. IV. 15. 11, Manu V. 84 (na vardhayed-aghÄhÄni) the word ‘agha’ has come to mean the same thing as ÄÅ›auca3. The PadmapurÄá¹‡a II. 66. 73-74 states that since the outlets of the body always emit mucus, urine &c. it is always impure.

The Mit. on YÄj. III. 1 defines4 ÄÅ›auca as an emergent attribute attaching to a person, which is got rid of by lapse of time or a bath and the like and which is the cause of the positive direction to offer to a deceased person) piá¹‡á¸a, water and the like and of the cessation of Vedic study and other actions. The Mit. adds that ÄÅ›auca is not merely the absence of the privilege or power to do religious acts, since even those who have incurred impurity (on death &c.) are enjoined to do certain religious acts such as offering water (to the deceased). This definition of the Mit, appears to be an echo of the Garudapuraá¹‡a ( pretakhaá¹‡á¸a) 5. 9 and is probably based on a verse of the work called Saá¹…graha. Haradatta5 on Gautama 14. 1 states that ÄÅ›auca may be defined as the absence of the privilege to perform religious acts, unfitness for being one whose food may be partaken of, untouchability and the loss of the privilege to make religious gifts. A comparatively early writer Bhaá¹­á¹­ÄcÄrya6, appears to have defined ‘Å›uddhi’ as the ‘removal of sin’ or as ‘being fit for performing religious acts’. This was accepted by the Smá¹›ti-candrikÄ, but rejected by Nandapaá¹‡á¸ita, commentator of á¹¢adaÅ›Ä«ti (pp. 2-3). Even the Mit. (on Yaj. III. 18) emphasizes that ÄÅ›aucas has two characteristics, viz. it takes away the privilege of performing religious acts and it renders a person untouchable. The Smá¹›timuktÄphala follows this.

Two characteristics of ÄÅ›auca

Rudradhara in his Åšuddhiviveka (D. C. ms, No. 309 of 1887-91 folio 1) says that Å›uddhi is a special attribute which brings about a capacity or privilege for the performance of all dharmas, while aÅ›uddhi is an attribute opposed to Å›uddhi and arises on the occasion of the birth of a sapiá¹‡á¸a or the like7.

Ä€Å›auca is of two kinds, viz, that arising on birth8 (and so called jananÄÅ›auca or sÅ«taka) and that arising on death (and therefore called Å›ÄvÄÅ›auca or má¹›takÄÅ›auca or maraá¹‡ÄÅ›auca). ÅšÄva is derived from Å›ava (meaning a corpse). The word sÅ«taka occurs in the9 Ait. Br. (chap. 32.8) and is there probably used in the sense of impurity on both birth and death. There it is said that if an ÄhitÄgni partook of food from the house of him who was affected by sÅ«taka, then the prÄyaÅ›citta was to offer a puroá¸ÄÅ›a cooked on eight potsherds to Agni Tantumat. Tantu means also ‘son or progeny’ and so it may be argued that it is an indication that sÅ«taka is used in the Ait. Br. in the sense of impurity on birth. The word sÅ«taka is used in the smá¹›tis in three senses:

  1. impurity on birth (vide Manu V. 58);
  2. impurity both on birth and death, as in Gobhilasmá¹›ti III. 6) and 63;10
  3. impurity on death alone (as in Daká¹£a VI. 1 and Gobhilasmá¹›ti III. 48 )11.

In some of the modern vernaculars (such as Marathi) the word “sÅ«taka’ is used in the 3rd sense alone. In each of these two divisions there are two varieties viz. where the duration is brief or comparatively long (á¹¢aá¸aÅ›iti, verse 2).

A question arises why birth and death should cause impurity to the members of the family or to relatives, Only a few have to say anything on this question, HÄrita says:

the family incurs death impurity because by death the family feels overwhelmed (or frustrated), while when a new life appears the family increases (and there is gratification or joy)12.

Conflict of smá¹›tis on ÄÅ›auca

The literature on Åšuddhi (including ÄÅ›auca) is very extensive. Apart from the sÅ«tras, smá¹›tis and PurÄá¹‡as there are numerous digests dealing with the subject at great length. Some of them have been printed. The smá¹›tis contain a mass of contradictory dicta to such an extent and different from the usages of medieval times that the Mit. on Yaj. III. 22 after quoting the views of ParÄÅ›ara, ÅšatÄtapa, Vasiá¹£á¹­ha and Aá¹…giras on the periods for which impurity had to be observed by persons belonging to the four vará¹‡as refuses to evolve order out of then and remarks that the usages in its day were different from the dicta of those sages13. The Madana-pÄrijÄta (p. 392) agrees with the Mit. and also suggests other modes of dealing with the conflict. Two examples may be cited of the way in which different smá¹›tis approach the same problem.

Exclusions for the learned

Atri 83, ParÄÅ›ara III, 5 and Daká¹£a VI, 6 provide that a brÄhmaá¹‡a who has consecrated the three Vedic fires and has mastered the Veda becomes free from impurity (on birth and death ) in one day, one who has merely mastered the Veda ( but has not consecrated the Å›rauta fires ) in three days and one who is devoid of both in ten days. Manu V.59 gives various options viz. 10 days, 4 days, 3 days, 1 day but does not state explicitly to whom his remarks apply.

Bá¹›haspati14 (q. by HÄralatÄ p. 5 and Haradatta on Gautama 14. 1) states that a brÄhmaá¹‡a who is endowed with Veda and Å›rauta fires becomes pure after three days, one who is devoid of Å›rauta fires (but is master of the Veda) in five days and one who is only a brÄhmaá¹‡a by caste (has not studied the Veda or does not teach it ) in ten days. The SÄn, Sr. and Manu15 (both quoted above) urge a man not to increase through laziness the number of days for which impurity is to be observed.

It is possible that persons claiming to be learned observed impurity for a few days only, while neighbours disputed their claim to this special dispensation in favour of Vedic learning and keeping Å›rauta fires. The result was that later on a flat number of ten days was prescribed for all brÄhmaá¹‡as whether learned or not and the provision for lesser number of days of impurity was relegated to Kalivarjya usages. Vide H. of Dh. vol. III, pp. 941-42.

Caste

Another example of the dependence of the days of impurity on the caste of the deceased and of varying dicta thereon may be cited.

  • Manu V. 83, Daká¹£a VI. 7, YÄj. III. 22, Atri (verse 85), Sankha (15. 2-3), MatsyapurÄá¹‡a (18. 2-3), Brahmapuraá¹‡a 220.63, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 22. 1-4, all lay down periods of 10 days, 12 days, 15 days and a month for impurity among brÄhmaá¹‡as, ká¹£atriyas, vaiÅ›yas and Å›Å«dras respectively.
  • Yaj. III.22 makes the virtuous Å›Å«dra observe impurity only for 15 days as a concession.
  • Gaut. (15.1-4) prescribes 10, 11, 12 days (or half month) and a month respectively for the four vará¹‡as, while Vas. (4.27-30) gives the periods of 10, 15, 20 and one month for the four vará¹‡as.
  • Prof. D. R. Bhandarkar, in his article on NÄgara brÄhmaá¹‡as and KÄyasthas of Bengal, protests that the KÄyasthas of Bengal (owing to social tyranny) still observe ÄÅ›auca for one month as if they are ordinary Å›Å«dras (I. A. for 1932 at p. 71).
  • On the other hand, Aá¹…giras ( quoted by the Mit, on Yaj. III, 22 ) states that Å›ÄtÄtapa declared that all vará¹‡as become purified after ten days whether the impurity is due to birth or death16.

sÄpiá¹‡á¸ya

ParÄÅ›ara (III. 9) quoted by the Mit. on YÄj. III, 18 provides that a sapiá¹‡á¸a who is 4th in descent from the common ancestor of the deceased and himself becomes pure after ten days, one who is 5th after 6 days, one who is 6th in descent after four days and one who is 7th after one day. The Mit. says that this should be discarded as it is in conflict with many other smá¹›tis and is disapproved of by people17.

Child death

The Mit, on Yaj. III, 18 declares that when a child that has not completed two years dies, it is the parents that have to observe ÄÅ›auca for ten days and not all sapiá¹‡á¸as and that their ÄÅ›auca entails untouchability for them.

VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara

Some of the rules mentioned by VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara (about 1100 A. D.) themselves underwent changes in 500 years as noted by the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (composed in 1612 A. D.18).

The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu (p. 517) remarks that what VijÃ±Änesvara said is not now (in its time) the usage and that the Smá¹›tyarthasÄra also did not accept VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara’s view.

Works on ÄÅ›auca

In view of the above-mentioned circumstances no useful purpose would be served by setting out in detail the varying dicta of the smá¹›tis, PurÄá¹‡as19 and the digests upon the several matters that fall to be treated under ÄÅ›auca. Digests on this subject are too numerous to mention. The following printed ones and a few mss, have been made use of in preparing the following account. In the first place, there are several works that deal with this subject in verse.

The AÅ›aucÄá¹£á¹­aka attributed to Vararuci (published in the Trivandrum series) with a commentary by an anonymous writer that names the bhÄá¹£ya of Maskarin on Gautama-dharmasÅ«tra (on p. 35) deals with the subject in eight sragdharÄ verses.

The AÅ›aucadaÅ›aka or DaÅ›aÅ›loki attributed to VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara seems to have been a very popular work. There are several commentaries on it. The one by Harihara is the earliest of them. In the Deccan College collection of Mss (now at the Bhandarkar Oriental Institute, Poona) there are several Mss of it, two of which were copied in saá¹vat 1539 (D. C. No. 216 of 1879-80) and saá¹vat 1579 (D.C, No. 196 of 1884-87) and which expressly state that the work was composed by VijÃ±ÄneÅ›vara-yogindra. The ten Å›ÄrdÅ«lavikridita verses of the AÅ›aucadaÅ›aka are set out in the appendix.

The Kalpataru of Laká¹£mÄ«dhara has a section on Å›uddhi which has been recently published in the G.O.S.

The HÄralatÄ of Aniruddha is very useful (B. I. Series).

The AÅ›aucakÄnda of the SmrÌ¥ticandrikÄ has been edited by the late Dr. Shamsastry and published as No. 56 of the Mysore University Sanskrit publications.

The TrÌ¥imÅ›acchloki with the commentary of Raghunatha composed in Å›ake 1645 has been published at the Poona Ä€nandaÅ›rama press and contains 30 SragdharÄ verses on ÄÅ›auca.

The á¹¢aá¸aÅ›Ä«ti ( 86 verses in the Anuá¹£á¹­ubh metre ) of KauÅ›ikÄditya with the commentary ÅšuddhicandrikÄ by VinÄyaka alias Nandapaá¹‡á¸ita (composed about 1600 A. D.) has been published in the Chowkhamba Sanskrit series.

The Åšuddhikaumudi of GovindÄnanda (B. I. Series ); the Åšuddhitattva of Raghunandana (ed. by Jivananda pp. 233-412), the ÅšuddhiprakÄÅ›a (part of the Viramitrodaya of MitramiÅ›ra ) published in the Chowkhamba Sanskrit series, the ÅšuddhimayÅ«kha of Nilakaá¹‡á¹­ha (edited by Mr. J. R. Gharpure) and the Smá¹›timuktÄphala of VaidyanÄtha (edited by Mr. J. R. Gharpure) are compilations that are useful in their own way.

These exhaustive treatises on ÄÅ›auca show one thing unmistakably that brÄhmaá¹‡as of the medieval ages attached an extremely exaggerated importance to ceremonial purity of the body on birth and death.

Periods

The periods of impurity depended on many circumstances. There was a difference between impurity on birth and that on death; so also the duration depended upon whether the deceased was an infant or a male or a female or one whose upanayana had been performed or not performed; it depended on caste and also on the question whether the deceased died near the relative or far away; also upon the degree of relationship; and on the time that elapsed from the birth or death till it reached the ears of the relative. The intensity of impurity was different in each of the following: a sÅ«tikÄ (a freshly delivered woman), rajasvalÄ (woman in monthly illness ), impurity on death, impurity on birth (the last being the least).

Daká¹£a (VI, 2-3) states that there are ten varieties in impurity, viz. immediate purity ( after taking a bath ), one day, three days, four days, six days, ten days, twelve days, a fortnight, a month and till the end of one’s life20. Daká¹£a then explains in what cases these different periods apply. As to what is meant by impurity till life’s end (lit, till he is reduced to ashes), Daká¹£a ( VI. 8-10 ) says:

’those who take their meals without bathing or offering to gods or without making gifts all such persons are in impurity till life ends.

One who is permanently afflicted with a disease,
who is close-fisted (i.e. stints himself, wife and son and religious acts through greed),
who is always in debt (i. e. who has not paid off his debts to gods, sages and manes),
who is bereft of religious acts ( nitya or naimittika),
who is a fool and is under the thumb of his wife,
whose mind is bent on vices (gambling, prostitution &c. ),
who is always dependent (a king’s servant or the like),
who is devoid of faith and benevolence (charitable gifts) -

he incurs impurity which ends only with his ashes (i.e, only when he is cremated)21.

These words are not to be taken literally; what is meant is that such a man is to be shunned by others (i.e, it is an arthavÄda conveying merely censure).

Impurity on birth

We shall now turn to the impurity on birth.

That the impurity on birth lasted for ten days even in Vedic times can be inferred from the story of Åšunaá¸¥-Å›epa narrated in the Ait. Br. (chapter 33, 2), where occurs the passage ‘when the animal is more than ten days from birth he becomes pure (and fit to be offered in sacrifice).’ The same inference can probably be drawn from Tai. Br, II, 1. 1.3, where it is said ’therefore people do not take the milk of a cow for ten nights when a calf is born’22.

srÄva, pÄta, prasava

Abortion in the first four months23 of pregnancy is called srÄva, abortion in the 5th or 6th month is called pÄta and from the 7th month of pregnancy onwards it is called prasÅ«ti or prasava ( according to ParÄÅ›ara III, 16, á¹¢adasiti verse 9).

srÄva and pÄta

When there is srÄva the mother incurs impurity for three days, in the case of pÄta the mother has to observe impurity for as many days as correspond to the months of pregnancy (i. e. 5 days or 6 days).

The impurity consists in the mother being untouchable. The father alone has to bathe when there is srÄva, but when there is pÄta the father and sapiá¹‡á¸as have to observe impurity for three days (according to Madana-pÄrijÄta p. 380-381 and others) but they don’t incur impurity as on death. These rules hold good for all castes.

prasava

But when the foetus comes out dead in any month from the 7th or the child is still-born, then the impurity is for ten days for both parents and the sapiá¹‡á¸as for all vará¹‡as or for 10, 12, 15 and 30 days respectively for the four vará¹‡as as laid down in YÄj. (III. 22 ) and the samÄnodakas have to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days and sagotras for one day (Dharmasindhu p. 427). The above rules are more or less laid down by Gautama 14. 15-16, Baud. Dh, S. I, 5, 136, ParÄÅ›ara III, 24, Manu V. 66, YÄj. III. 20 (latter half) and the first verse of Ä€Å›aucadaÅ›aka.

The mother is untouchable for ten days on birth, still-birth or abortion in 7th, 8th or 9th month but the father and sapiá¹‡á¸as24 when they take a bath after the prasava are not untouchable (Yaj. III. 19).

In ancient times there were several differing views about jananÄÅ›auca for the father as vouched for by Baud25 Dh. S. I. 5. 125-128.

Although a woman becomes touchable in ten days after delivery she is not fit to take part in religious rites for 20 days after she becomes touchable (i. e. 30 days from birth ) if she gave birth to a son but for 30 days (i.e. 40 days from birth) if a daughter was born26. Women of all vará¹‡as become pure (i.e. touchable) ten days after delivery–says Pracetas27. Devala states that there is no ÄÅ›auca on the ground of birth when the period of (ten or twelve ) days has expired28.

If a woman was delivered at her father’s or brother’s house, then her parents and her brothers staying with the father had to observe ÄÅ›auca for one day (Dharmasindhu p. 427), but if a woman was delivered of a child at her husband’s house then her father or brother had to observe no ÄÅ›auca (on birth). Even when sagotras have to observe ÄÅ›auca on birth they are not untouchable (á¹¢adaÅ›Ä«ti verse 6).

General rules about ÄÅ›auca

It would be better to state here certain general rules once for all. When any text employs the word day (ahaá¸¥) or night (rÄtri) in prescribing the duration of ÄÅ›auca what is meant is ahorÄtra (both day29 and night). When counting the days of aÅ›auca one has to begin from the day of the cremation, if the deceased was an ÄhitÄgni, but from the day of death in the case of one not an ÄhitÄgni30 ( Ä€Å›auca-daÅ›aka verse 4, KÅ«rma, UttarÄrdha 23.52).

Birth and death are causes of ÄÅ›auca only when they are known to the person who is to be affected by it and not by the mere fact of the birth or death.

This follows from certain passages of PÄraskara gr. III. 10 and Manu V. 75-76 and the BrahmapurÄá¹‡a. PÄraskara (III. 10) provides :31

‘if one who had gone abroad dies,
(his relatives) should on hearing (of his death) sit down and offer him water and should remain untouchable for the days that remain out of the proper period (viz. 10, 12, 15, 30 days);
if the prescribed period of aÅ›auca ) has expired they should observe ÄÅ›auca for one night or three nights,’

Manu (V.75-76) is to the same effect. The BrahmapurÄá¹‡a states:

‘if a donor makes a gift and the acceptor accepts it when both are not aware of there being a birth or death in their family, no blame attaches.’

Rules about ÄÅ›auca on death

We should now turn to ÄÅ›auca on death. Here again there is no unanimity and therefore the views preferably of the latest works (such as the Dharmasindhu) will be set out after referring to a few smá¹›ti passages. Ä€Å›auca on death renders those who incur it untouchable and unable to perform religious rites. PÄr. gr. III, 10. 29-30 (S. B. E. vol. 29 p. 357) stated generally that the impurity caused by death lasts through three nights and that according to some teachers through ten nights.

  • If a child dies within ten days of birth, the father and mother have to observe JananÄÅ›auca and become purified by the end of the ÄÅ›auca on birth and the father remains untouchable for those days (KÅ«rma-purÄá¹‡a q. by S. K, p. 21).
  • If a child died before it struck teeth the sapiá¹‡á¸as had only to undergo a bath, while the parents had to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days if the child was a son and for one day if a daughter (vide YÄj. III. 23, Sankha 15. 4, Atri 95, Ä€Å›aucadaÅ›aka verse 2).
  • If the child died after striking teeth but before CÅ«dÄ (or the end of the third year) the sapiá¹‡á¸as had to observe ÄÅ›auca for one day and night (Yaj. III. 23, Sankha 15, 5), but the parents had ÄÅ›auca (of death) for three days. If the child was a girl the sapiá¹‡á¸as became pure by a bath up to the girl’s third year.
  • If death occurs between CÅ«dÄ (or three years) and upanayana or marriage (in the case of girls) the sapiá¹‡á¸as including the father have to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days and samÄnodakas become purified by a bath alone.
  • After upanayana all sapiá¹‡á¸as have to observe ÄÅ›auca (on death ) for ten days (Gaut. 14, 1, Manu V. 59, AÅ›aucadaÅ›aka 2) and all samÄnodakas for three days.

  • In the case of a Å›Å«dra dying after three and before marriage or 16 years the ÄÅ›auca is three days for all sapiá¹‡á¸as.

  • After sixteen years or marriage (in the case of a Å›Å«dra) it is the ÄÅ›auca prescribed for his caste.

  • In the case of a girl dying after three years and before vÄgdÄna (betrothal) the parents have ÄÅ›auca for three days and the sapipdas (up to three generations ) one day.

  • If a girl dies after vÄgdÄna and before marriage the sapiá¹‡á¸as of her father as well as of the proposed bridegroom had to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days.

  • In the case of women and Å›Å«dras if death takes place after marriage or after 16 years (if the SÅ«dra was unmarried) the period of ÄÅ›auca for all sapiá¹‡á¸as is ten days.

  • If a woman dies at her father’s house after marriage, her parents, step-mother, full brothers and step-brothers have to obserye ÄÅ›auca for three days and her paternal uncle and the like that stay in the same house with her father for one day.

  • Some say that if the married daughter dies in a village other than that of the father, the parents have to observe ÄÅ›auca for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« 32 (i.e. two nights with a day between or two days with a night between). There are other views on this point which are passed over. For example, Viá¹£á¹‡u-dharmasÅ«tra (22. 32-34) says that in the case of a married woman there is no ÄÅ›auca on the parents’ side; but when she is delivered of a child or dies in the father’s house then the impurity is for one day or three days respectively33.

  • A married woman has to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days for the death of her parents or step-mother if ten days have not elapsed from the day of death or for the remaining days out of the period of ten (Yaj. III, 21 latter half).

  • If the married daughter hears of the death of the parents or step-mother at a time more than ten days after the death or within one year she has to observe ÄÅ›auca for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä«.

  • If a brother whose upanayana has been performed dies at his married sister’s house or vice versa, ÄÅ›auca has to be observed for three days by them, but if they do not die at each other’s house but in a different one then the ÄÅ›auca is for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« and if the death occurs in another village then there is ÄÅ›auca only for one day.

  • The same rules apply to step-brothers and step-sisters and also among sisters.
  • A married woman on the death of her paternal grand-father or paternal uncle has to undergo only a bath.
  • If a maternal uncle dies, the nephew and the niece have to observe aÅ›auca for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä«.
  • If the maternal uncle dies in the house of the nephew then there is ÄÅ›auca for three days and for only one day if the maternal uncle had not his upanayana performed or died in another village; the same rules apply to one’s mother’s step-brother.
  • If the wife of a maternal uncle dies, the nephew or the niece of the maternal uncle have to undergo ÄÅ›auca for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä«.
  • If a nephew whose thread ceremony has been performed dies, his maternal uncle and maternal aunt have ÄÅ›auca for three days.
  • The same rules apply if the deceased be the son of a step-sister of the maternal uncle.
  • If the niece (sister’s daughter) dies there is only a bath for the maternal uncle.
  • If a maternal grandfather dies, the grandchild (the son or daughter of a daughter) has to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days, but only for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« if the maternal grandfather dies in a different village.
  • On the death of the maternal grandmother the grandchildren have to observe aÅ›auca for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä«, Some works do not prescribe ÄÅ›auca for a female descendant such as a niece or grand-daughter.
  • If a daughter’s son, whose upanayana had been performed, died, the maternal grandfather and grandmother had to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days and for paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« if the daughter’s son had not upanayana performed for him.
  • On the death of a daughter’s daughter the maternal grandfather and mother incur no ÄÅ›auca.

In all these matters the general rule is that a male whose upanayana had been performed and a married woman alone are liable to undergo aÅ›auca for any relative other than the parents (i. e, on the death of a parent a male though without upanayana and a woman though unmarried are liable to undergo ÄÅ›auca).

  • If a man’s father-in-law or mother-in-law die near (in the house of) the man, he has to undergo ÄÅ›auca for three days, but only for paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« if the death is elsewhere than with the son-in-law.

  • On the death of a son-in-law, the father-in-law and the mother-in-law have to observe ÄÅ›auca for one day or they have only to take a bath, but if the son-in-law dies in the house of the father-in-law then the ÄÅ›auca is for three days.

  • On the death of one’s wife’s brother, the ÄÅ›auca is for one day if upanayana had been performed; but if no upanayana was performed or if he died in another village a mere bath is enough.

  • On the death of one’s mother’s sisters (full or half) the person (whether man or woman) has to undergo ÄÅ›auca for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä«; the same rule applies on the death of the father’s sister.

  • If it is a step-sister of the father then a mere bath is enough. The father’s sister has to undergo a bath if the nephew dies. If one’s father’s sister or mother’s sister dies in one’s house then the ÄÅ›auca is for three days.

  • In the case of bandhus (described by the Mit. on YÄj.   • If the daughter of the father’s sister (and of the other bandhus enumerated in the three verses) dies married, the ÄÅ›auca is one day, but if they die unmarried only a bath is necessary.

  • Among the three kinds of bandhus, a man himself and his three Ätmabandhus have to observe ÄÅ›auca for each other’s death; but the case is different as to pitá¹›bandhus and mÄtá¹›bandhus.

  • If any one of these latter dies, there is ÄÅ›auca for the man whose bandhus they are, but if a man dies, his pitá¹›bandhus and mÄtá¹›bandhus have to observe no ÄÅ›auca.

  • If an adopted son dies, his natural father and adoptive father have to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days (the VyavahÄramayÅ«kha differs from this ) and the sapiá¹‡á¸as for one day.

  • If the adoptive or natural father dies, the adopted son has to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days and for one day for the sapiá¹‡á¸as of any one of them.

  • On the death of the son and the grandson of an adopted son, the sapiá¹‡á¸as of the natural father and the adoptive father have to observe ÄÅ›auca for one day and vice versa.

These rules apply if the son adopted is not a sapiá¹‡á¸a or samÄnodaka of the adopter, when he was in his family of birth. But if a sagotra sapiá¹‡á¸a or a samÄnodaka be adopted, then the ÄÅ›auca is for ten or three days respectively.

  • If one’s ÄcÄrya35 dies, the pupil has to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days, but only one day if he dies in another village (Gautama 14, 26, 52, Manu V. 80).
  • On the death of the ÄcÄrya’s wife or son the ÄÅ›auca is for one day36.
  • On the death of a guru (who instructs in Vedic mantras) the ÄÅ›auca is three days and paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« if he dies in another village.
  • On the death of a teacher who imparted instruction in grammar, astronomy and other aá¹…gas (subsidiary lores) of the Veda, the ÄÅ›auca is one day.
  • Similar rules are laid down about the death of a pupil, á¹›tvik (sacrificial priest), a sacrificer, a dependent Å›rotriya, a fellow-student, a friend, which are all passed over as not now useful. Vide Gaut, 14. 19-20 (which prescribe one day’s ÄÅ›auca on the death of a fellow-student or a dependent Å›rotriya).

These provisions about ÄÅ›auca on the death of the ÄcÄrya and rÌ¥tvik who were not sapiá¹‡á¸as show how closely the bond between teacher and pupil was thought to be in ancient times, almost equalling the tie of blood.

  • When a yati (ascetic) dies,37 all his sapiá¹‡á¸as have simply to undergo a bath (and nothing more).
  • Conversely, a yati and brahmacÄrin have to observe no ÄÅ›auca. Manu V. 82, YÄj. III. 25, Viá¹£á¹‡u 22.45.
  • Sankha 15.15 provide that on the death of the ruler of the country in which one resides one has to observe ÄÅ›auca up till the coming of the day or night next to that on which the king dies38.

  • As long as a corpse is not removed from the village in which a man dies, the whole village is in ÄÅ›auca. Ä€.p. Dh. S. 1. 3. 9. 14 declares that there is no Veda study till a corpse lies in the village.

  • The Smá¹›ti-muktÄphala (p. 541) quotes39 several smá¹›tis to the effect that in a village in which a corpse lies unremoved, there is to be no eating, no Vedic study and no sacrifice, but that this does not apply where in a village there are more than four hundred brÄhmaá¹‡as.   • The ideas of ritualistic purity went so far as to provide (as stated in the Åšuddhitattva quoted by the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu) that if a dog died in a brÄhmaá¹‡a’s house the house became impure for ten days, that, if a Å›Å«dra40, a patita or a mleccha died in a brÄhmaá¹‡a’s house, the house became impure for a month, two months or four months respectively and the house had to be abandoned if a Å›vapÄka died therein.+++(5)+++

AtikrÄntÄÅ›auca

AtikrÄntÄÅ›auca (impurity on coming to know of birth or death after the several periods fixed). The general rule41 is that, if a person stays in a different country and hears of a birth or death among his sapiá¹‡á¸as, he has to observe ÄÅ›auca not for ten days after hearing but for that number of days that are short of the ten days (counting from birth or death) prescribed for sapiá¹‡á¸as. Vide Manu V. 75, YÄj. III. 21 (latter half), Åšaá¹…kha 15.11, PÄr. gr. III. 10.

Ä€Å›auca interferes very much with one’s activities and it is hence that often-times people send a closed letter to their relatives living in a different place stating thereon that it be opened on a particular day (which is the 10th day from the death of a sapiá¹‡á¸a). Every one knows the meaning of such a direction and by such a subterfuge inconvenience is avoided and the dictates of the Å›Ästra are deemed to be satisfied.

If a son hears of the death of a parent he has to observe ÄÅ›auca for ten days from the day of hearing, but if he hears of the death of a parent before the collection of bones then he has to observe ÄÅ›auca only for the days that remain (Smá¹›ti-muktÄphala p. 534).

There is great divergence about the period of ÄÅ›auca if the death of a sapiá¹‡á¸a comes to one’s ears more than ten days after the day of death. Manu V.77 provides that on hearing of the death of a sapiá¹‡á¸a or the birth of a son after ten days from the event) a man becomes pure after plunging into water with the clothes on and YÄj. From the words of Manu that only the father, even if he hears of the birth of a son more than ten days after the event, has to take a bath the Mit. infers that there is no atikrÄntÄÅ›auca for sapiá¹‡á¸as on birth42. The Mit. quotes Devala in support (vide note 621 above).

The Dharmasindhu follows the Mit. Manu V. 76, Åšaá¹…kha 15.12, KÅ«rmapurÄá¹‡a (UttarÄrdha) 23,21 state that when ten days have passed after the event of death, the man who hears of the death is impure for three days and if he hears of the death more than a year after death, he becomes pure after a bath.

In order to remove the conflict among smá¹›tis Vá¹›ddha-Vasiá¹£á¹­ha lays down: ‘ÄÅ›auca is for three days if the news of death is heard within three months (but later than ten days after death), for a paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« if it is heard within six months (but later than three months), for one day if heard before nine months (but later than 6 months ) and thereafter (i.e, up to one year but later than nine months ) one becomes pure by a bath. But the Mit, says43 that this holds good as to all except one’s parents and quotes Paiá¹­hinasi and another smá¹›ti for the proposition that whenever a son staying in a distant country hears of the death of a parent whether within a year or after a year he has to observe ÄÅ›auca for ten days from the day he becomes aware of it. The Dharmasindhu (p. 433 ) remarks that there is no atikrÄntÄÅ›auca as regards the periods of one day and three days prescribed on the death of one whose upanayana had not been performed or as regards the periods of paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« and three days on the death of the maternal uncle and others of a different gotra.

Similarly, there is no atikrÄntÄÅ›auca with reference to the ÄÅ›auca for three days prescribed in the case of samÄnodakas; but in these cases even after the lapse of the fixed time a bath is necessary. The rules about atikrÄntÄÅ›auca apply only to ÄÅ›auca on death for ten days. As in the case of the son, so in the cases of husband and wife and of co-wives among themselves even after the lapse of more than a year and even if the death is in a different country the husband or wife or co-wife has to observe ÄÅ›auca for ten days. The parents, even when they hear of the death of an aurasa son after a year, have to observe ÄÅ›auca for three days. On the news of the death of a sapiá¹‡á¸a living in the same country coming to a sapiá¹‡á¸a’s ears after ten days but up to three months, the period of aÅ›auca is three days, paká¹£ipi up to 6 months, up to nine months one day and up to one year a bath. Here also there are various views such as those of MÄdhava and others. Vide ÅšuddhiprakÄÅ›a pp. 49-51 for various views.

AÅ›auca if death in another country

The Mit on YÄj. III. 21 (latter half ) makes a distinction between
knowledge of the death of a sapiá¹‡á¸a staying in the same country coming to a person’s ears after ten days from death
and of the death of a sapiá¹‡á¸a staying in another country separated by a big river &c.
In the latter case the sapiá¹‡á¸as coming to hear of the death after ten days but even before three months are purified by a mere bath. It quotes44 a smá¹›ti verse ‘on hearing that a person (sapiá¹‡á¸a) died in another country and in the case of the death of an impotent person or a forest hermit or an ascetic purification follows after a bath and the same applies to sagotra sapiá¹‡á¸as in the case of abortion,’ The á¹¢aá¸aÅ›Ä«ti (35) has a similar verse45. The Mit. quotes two verses of Bá¹›haspati which define what deÅ›Äntara (a different country ). means:46 Where there is a large river or there is a mountain which separates (one territory from another ) or where the languages differ then there is deÅ›Äntara. Some say that a different country means the distance of sixty yojanas, while others put it down at forty yojanas and still others at thirty yojanas.’ There is a difference of opinion as to whether in order to constitute a different country all three must co-exist (viz, a large river, a mountain and difference of speech) or any one of the three will suffice or whether 60, 40 or 30 yojanas would constitute a different country or whether the requirement of ten days for news to be carried to a place would constitute deÅ›Äntara. The Sm. C. and á¹¢adaÅ›iti 37 hold the view that any one of the above three is sufficient while others hold other views. The Åšuddhiviveka47 holds that a distance of 60 yojanas constituted deÅ›Äntara by itself, but even within sixty yojanas if a large river, a mountain and difference of speech occurred together, that could give rise to deÅ›Äntara.

The Smá¹›tyarthasÄra48 avers that deÅ›Äntara is differently described in the Smá¹›tis, PurÄá¹‡as and the works on tirthas. For yojana, vide H. of Dh. vol. III n. 185 pp. 145-146.

The Dharmasindhu (p. 435) provides that if the bones of an ÄhitÄgni were not found and only an effigy of palÄÅ›a leaves were burnt, still the ÄÅ›auca is for ten days even if he died in a different country and a long time had elapsed. Similarly, in the case of the effigy of one who is not an ÄhitÄgni, the ÄÅ›auca is for ten days for his son and wife if they have not already observed any ÄÅ›auca for him but if they had observed it (on hearing of his death ) then for three days (on the burning of the effigy ). For other sapiá¹‡á¸as (than the son and wife ) in the same circumstances three days and bath are respectively prescribed.

It is provided in the Gá¹›hya kÄrikas, the Smá¹›tyarthasÄra (p. 94), Dharmasindhu (p. 435) and other works that if a man49 went to a distant country and no news of his being alive was heard, then his son or other relative), after the lapse of 20 years from the time when no news was heard if the man went away when he was young or 15 years if he left when he was of middle age or 12 years if he left later in life, should perform three cÄndrÄyaá¹‡as or thirty ká¹›cchras, prepare an effigy of the man with kuÅ›as (or with palÄÅ›a leaves ), should burn it and then observe ÄÅ›auca and perform Å›rÄddhas &c.

Summary of rules on ÄÅ›auca

To summarize the above discussion it is clear that as indicated by MedhÄtithi on Manu V. 58 there is a distinction as regards the periods of aÅ›auca and the persons affected by it in several ways.

  1. There is a distinction between ÄÅ›auca on birth and ÄÅ›auca on death;
  2. As to aÅ›auca on death numerous distinctions arise viz. a) when there is abortion (garbhasrÄva or garbhapÄta, as in Sankha 15. 4, Bá¹›hat-ParÄÅ›ara VI. p. 186 );
    b) when the foetus comes out from the 7th to the 9th month of pregnancy or the child is still-born or dies after being born but before the appearance of teeth (YÄj. III. 23, Atri 95);
    c) after the appearance of teeth but before cÅ«á¸Ä or three years (Viá¹£á¹‡u 22. 29, YÄj. III. 23);
    d) after cÅ«á¸Ä or three years up to upanayana (Manu V. 67);
    e) after upanayana (Yaj. III, 23, Manu V. 59, Gaut. 14. 1);
    f) as to ÄÅ›auca on death after upanayana the duration at one time depended in the case of brÄhmaá¹‡as on Vedic study, the performance of Å›rauta rites and whether the brÄhmaá¹‡a subsisted on ears of corn left in the field after the crops were harvested50 (ParÄÅ›ara TII, 5, Sankha 15.1, Atri 83, AgnipurÄá¹‡a 158. 10-11);
    g) the duration of ÄÅ›auca depended on caste (Gaut. 14. 1-4, YÄj. III. 22 &c. );
    h) the duration of ÄÅ›auca depended on nearness of blood i.e. whether the person affected was a sapiá¹‡á¸a or a samÄnodaka (Gaut. 14. 1 and 18, Manu V. 59, 64);
    i) the duration depended also on the nearness or distance from the place of death (Laghu-Ä€Å›valÄyana 20. 85, 89);
    j) it also depended on whether the deceased died in a different country separated by a large river, a mountain or thirty yojanas (Laghu-Ä€Å›valÄyana 20.87);
    k) duration depended on the time that elapsed before it came to the knowledge of the relative;
    l) the duration depended upon whether another ÄÅ›auca supervened when the period of the first was not over.

If a person is born or died at night or news thereof is received at night, a question arises as to from what day the periods of ÄÅ›auca are to be calculated. For example, if a man died at 1 A. M. on Monday, was Monday to be included in counting ten days of ÄÅ›auca or was it to be excluded ? There are two views on this point51. One view is that any time before midnight is to be counted as belonging to the previous day, while any time after midnight is to be taken as belonging to the next day. On this view in the above illustration Monday would be excluded in the calculation of ten days. The other view is that the night is to be divided into three parts and if death occurs in the first two of the three parts then the day is to be included in the calculation; if the time of death falls in the 3rd part, the ten days are to be calculated from the next day. On this view Monday would be included in the ten days. The Dharmasindhu (p. 435 ) remarks that in this matter the usage of the country is to be followed. Vide MadanapÄrijÄta pp. 394-395 also.

Several rules are mentioned in the smá¹›tis about the periods of ÄÅ›auca for the relatives of higher castes when they marry women of lower vará¹‡as (i.e. when there are anuloma marriages). Corpse carrying

It has already been stated (p. 215) how it was the duty of sapiá¹‡á¸as to take out the corpse and to cremate it and how carrying the dead body of a poor brÄhmaá¹‡a was highly eulogised (ParÄÅ›ara III. 39-40). If a person carried a dead body through greed (for money ), a brÄhmaá¹‡a, kÅ›atriya, vaiÅ›ya or Å›Å«dra had to observe ÄÅ›auca respectively for 10, 12, or 15 days or a month. ÄÅ›auca cause separation

Persons58 undergoing an ÄÅ›auca on birth or death for one person were forbidden to touch other persons undergoing aÅ›auca for the birth or death of another person altogether. If they did so they had to perform prÄyaÅ›citta (PrÄjÄpatya or SÄntapana).

Exceptions to Ä€Å›auca

Certain exceptions were recognized to the above rules about partaking of food at the house of one who is afflicted with ÄÅ›auca.

Post-commencement

While a marriage59 rite (as also caula and upanayana ), festival in honour of a deity and a sacrifice (like Jyotiá¹£á¹­oma ) are in progress, if impurity due to birth or death overtakes the performer, the materials (money and other things ) already set apart for them may be dedicated or donated to the deity or brÄhmaá¹‡as and no fault is incurred.

As regards food, a smá¹›ti text60 quoted by the Mit. says

‘if in marriage, in a festival in honour of a deity or in a sacrifice ÄÅ›auca on birth or death intervenes, the cooked food should be served through others (not affected by the ÄÅ›auca) and the donor and the partaker of the food incur no blame.’

Acc. to Aá¹…giras61, Paiá¹­hinasi (q. by Sm. C.) and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S., when once a sacrifice (like a SomayÄga), a marriage, a sacrifice or festival in honour of the mother Goddesses or a deity or the dedication or foundation of an idol or temple has been begun a supervening ÄÅ›auca does not matter.

Even in modern times this is followed as regards upanayana and marriage. When exactly a sacrifice or marriage ceremony may be said to have begun is laid down by Laghu-Viá¹£á¹‡u as follows:62

  • in the case of a sacrifice it may be said to begin when the priests are chosen,
  • in the case of vratas and japa when the saá¹…kalpa (declaration) is made,
  • in the case of marriage when the NÄndiÅ›rÄddha is performed,
  • and in the case of a Å›rÄddha when the food meant for the brÄhmaá¹‡as has been cooked completely.

Things which can be taken

Certain things could be taken from the house of one who was undergoing ÄÅ›auca63 on birth or death and who was the owner thereof (though not actually from his hand, but with his permission). The KÅ«rmapurÄá¹‡a enumerates such articles. They are “fruits, flowers, raw vegetables, salt, firewood, butter-milk, curds, ghee, oil, drugs, milk and dry food” (like laddus, lÄjas ).

MarÄ«ci (q. by Mit. on Yaj. III. 17) and TrimÅ›ac-chloki verse 20 present oven longer lists of such things.

Some smá¹›tis and commentators lay down rules about the aÅ›auca to be observed by slaves when their masters are in ÄÅ›auca. Vide Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 22.19, Devalasmá¹›ti verse 6, Bá¹›haspati quoted by Haradatta on Gaut. 14.4. But as slavery has long been abolished this topic is passed over here.

Ä€Å›aucasannipÄta

Ä€Å›aucasannipÄta or ÄÅ›aucasampÄta (the knowledge of another ÄÅ›auca coming to a person already observing one ÄÅ›auca)64. The rules about this matter were promulgated early enough and they are based on convenience and common sense. The rules try to give relief to persons who may be sorely tried if it were held that, when another ÄÅ›auca supervenes while a person is already in the midst of one, he has to finish all the days of the first ÄÅ›auca and then begin the period of the second ÄÅ›auca. The Gaut. Dh..S. 14.5 starts by saying that if another ÄÅ›auca supervenes on one already being undergone, purification results after the remaining days of the first ÄÅ›auca are over. Then sÅ«tras 6 and 7 provide that if the second aÅ›auca supervenes in the last night of the first ÄÅ›auca then purification results in two days after the first ÄÅ›auca ends and if the second ÄÅ›auca comes to be known in the last watch of the night of the last day of the first ÄÅ›auca, the second ends in three days after the first ends. Baud. Dh. S. I. 5, 123 appears to be similar to Gaut. 14.5-6 Manu V. 79, YÄj. III. 20, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 22. 35-38, Åšaá¹…kha 15. 10, ParÄÅ›ara III. 28 lay down the general rule promulgated by Gautama 14.5.

Rules on ÄÅ›aucasannipÄta

Some general rules concerning this topic may first be stated.

The first rule is that in a conflict between ÄÅ›auca on death and ÄÅ›auca on birth, that on death is stronger65. The 2nd rule is that if both ÄÅ›aucas are of the same kind and the second is of the same duration or of lesser duration than the first then a man gets rid of both at the end of the first, but if the second, though of the same kind, has a longer duration than the first, then purification follows at the end of the one that is of longer duration. It has to be remembered that birth and death give rise to ÄÅ›auca66 only when they are known by a person.

On this topic there is a good deal of divergence between the views of the followers of the Mitaká¹£arÄ, of the Gaudas and Maithilas (vide ÅšuddhiprakÄsa pp. 74-82, Nirá¹‡ayasindhu 536-540). Following the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu it may be said that twelve alternatives are possible, when another ÄÅ›auca supervenes on an already existing one. They are set out here. (1 & 2) If both ÄÅ›aucas are due to birth and the second is of the same duration as the first or of lesser duration, then at the end of the first ÄÅ›auca there is purification from both (Visá¹‡u 22.35, Sankha 15.70); (3) If both ÄÅ›aucas are due to birth and the second one is of longer duration than the first then purification results at the end of the second (Åšankha 15.10, á¹¢adaÅ›iti 19 ); ( 4 and 5) If both ÄÅ›aucas arise on death and the second is of the same duration as the first or of lesser duration, then at the end of the period of the first aÅ›auca there is purification from both; (6) If both ÄÅ›aucas arise on death and the second that intervenes is of longer duration than the first then purification results at the end of the second (á¹¢adaÅ›Ä«ti 21); (7, 8 and 9) When the firt ÄÅ›auca is one arising from birth and an ÄÅ›auca due to deaths intervenes, the ÄÅ›auca on death must run its full course (i.e. there is no purification by the lapse of the first ÄÅ›auca due to birth), whether the ÄÅ›auca for death is of lesser duration than the ÄÅ›auca on birth, or whether it is of the same or of longer duration (á¹¢adaÅ›iti 18); (10, 11) If the67 first ÄÅ›auca arises on death and the supervening one is due to birth and is of lesser duration than the one on death or of the same duration then both end at the expiration of the first ÄÅ›auca on death (á¹¢adasÄ«ti 21); (12) If the first ÄÅ›auca arises on death and the supervening one arises on birth and is of longer duration, then both run their proper courses (á¹¢adaÅ›Ä«ti 21).

The Dharmasindhu (p. 436) generally follows the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu but remarks:

‘Ä€Å›auca on death cannot be done away with by ÄÅ›auca on birth which is of the same or greater duration; ÄÅ›auca on death of the extent of paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« cannot get rid of ÄÅ›auca on birth which is three or ten days in duration and ÄÅ›auca on birth extending to ten days cannot be got rid of by ÄÅ›auca on death which is of three days’ duration’.

These are the views of many writers. A certain writer says that ÄÅ›auca on birth though longer in duration can be got rid of by ÄÅ›auca on death which is of lesser extent.

The Mit. on YÄj. III, 20 (first half) states an exception to the above rules about ÄÅ›auca-sannipÄta. When a person’s mother dies and then, while the period of ÄÅ›auca for the mother’s death is not over, the father dies, it does not follow that the ÄÅ›auca for the father comes to an end with the end of the ÄÅ›auca for the mother, but the son has to observe the full period of68 the ÄÅ›auca for father’s death. Similarly, if the father died first and then, while the period of ÄÅ›auca for the father was not over, the mother died, it does not follow that the ÄÅ›auca for the mother’s death comes to an end with the end of the first period of ÄÅ›auca for the father, but the son after observing the period of the father’s ÄÅ›auca has to observe ÄÅ›auca for the mother for one paká¹£iá¹‡Ä« in addition. It may be remarked that AparÄrka construes this verse differently by stating that if the father dies during the period of ÄÅ›auca for the death of the mother, then the general rule applies, viz, that purification follows at the end of the ÄÅ›auca for the mother.

Special rules about ÄÅ›auca

If, while an ÄÅ›auca on death is being undergone, an ÄÅ›auca on birth arises, the father of the child born is able to perform the rites of JÄtakarma and the like, because he is to be deemed to be purified for the nonce, according to PrajÄpati69.

The á¹¢adaÅ›iti (verse 22) provides70 that the rule about purification on the expiry of the first ÄÅ›auca in the case of succeeding ÄÅ›aucas on birth or death has three exceptions, viz, a woman who is delivered of a child, the person who actually cremates the body (of the deceased) and the sons of the deceased; that is, a sÅ«tikÄ has to observe the proper period of untouchability and one who cremates a dead body has to observe ÄÅ›auca for ten days, though the delivery or cremation takes place in the midst of an ÄÅ›auca on death.

SADYAá¸¤-ÅšAUCA. (purification on the same day)

It has already been seen (p. 274) that Daká¹£a (VI. 2) speaks of ten kinds of impurity on birth or death, the first two of which are sadyaá¸¥Å›Äuca and ekÄha. EkÄha means day and night together (vide note 622 above). The ordinary meaning of ‘sadyaá¸¥’ is ‘at once, immediately.’ But when Yaj. III. 29, ParÄÅ›ara III. 10, Atri 97 and other smá¹›tis employ the word ‘sadyaá¸¥-Å›auca’ what is meant is that there is no ÄÅ›auca for a whole day or three days or ten days, but only up to the taking of a bath or up to the end of the day or the end of the night on which the event occurs. Panini71 mentions ‘sadyaá¸¥’ and thirteen other words in one sutra as nipÄtas (irregularly formed). From YÄj. III. 23 (Ädantajanmanaá¸¥ sadya ÄcÅ«dÄnnaiÅ›jki smá¹›tÄ) it impliedly follows that sadyaá¸¥ has the meaning of a part of a day or a part of the night’ (as the case may be) and ’naiÅ›iki’ means a whole day and night (as note 622 shows).

The Åšuddhitattva (pp. 340-341 ) explains72 ‘sadyaá¸¥’ as meaning ‘a portion of the day or of the night’ and cites several authorities in support.

The ÅšuddhiprakÄÅ›a (p. 92) explains that ‘sadyaá¸¥Å›auca’ in some contexts means ‘absence of ÄÅ›auca,’ while in others it means ‘bath’ and with reference to those killed in battle and the like (to whom piá¹‡á¸as have to be offered) it means a part of the day or of the night.’ The Smá¹›timuktÄphala73 (ÄÅ›aucakÄá¹‡á¸a ) p.481 says that’sadyaá¸¥Å›auca’ means ‘impurity that comes to an end by a bath.’ That piá¹‡á¸as have to be offered to those about whom there is ‘sadyaá¸¥Å›auca’ is stated by the AdipurÄá¹‡a74.

According to the Åšuddhikaumudi (p.73) ‘sadyaá¸¥Å›auca’ has two meanings, viz. (1) the entire absence of ÄÅ›auca as in the cases of sacrificial priests &c. (YÄj. III. 28), (2) ÄÅ›auca that is removed by a mere bath ( as in Manu V. 76).

The rules about ÄÅ›auca for several days do not operate in five classes of cases, viz.

  • certain persons are exempted altogether,
  • certain activities of certain persons who would ordinarily be untouchable owing to ÄÅ›auca are allowed without causing pollution,
  • certain articles can be taken by anybody without fear of pollution from those who are affected by ÄÅ›auca,
  • no ÄÅ›auca is to be observed for certain deceased persons owing to their faults,
  • in the case of certain persons express texts say that no ÄÅ›auca need be observed for them.

These five classes will briefly be dealt with in order. In the principal texts these five classes of cases are rather mixed up.

Exempt people

In the Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a III, 13.7 it is provided that there is sadyaá¸¥Å›auca on the death of a child, or of a person dying in another country, of a patita, or of an ascetic, or on the death of a person committing suicide by water, fire or hanging. Vide also Gaut. 14.11 and 42 and VÄmanapurÄá¹‡a 14.99.

YÄj. (III. 28-29 ) prescribes that there is sadyaá¸¥Å›auca (purification after a bath ) in case of birth or death of a sapiá¹‡da )

The second class of exceptions refers to cases where certain persons, though they have incurred ÄÅ›auca, are allowed to do certain acts or carry on their activities without bringing pollution to those with whom they deal or come in contact. For example, ParÄÅ›ara (III. 20-21) says;

Though suicide was generally condemned, the smá¹›tis (such as Atri 218-219) and PurÄá¹‡as allowed certain exceptions, viz. very old men (above 70), very weak men who cannot observe the rules of bodily purification or those who have no desire left for the pleasures of the senses or who have carried out all tasks and duties, may start on MahÄprasthÄna or die at PrayÄga.
